==PAGE:3==
Preface
This master’s thesis was prepared at the Department of Health Technology at the Technical University
of Denmark. The thesis started the 1st of March and ended the 22nd of August 2022. The thesis is
the result of 30 ECTS points - resulting in the fulfillment of the requirements for acquiring a master’s
degree in Biomedical Engineering.
Kongens Lyngby, August 22, 2022
Teitur Óli Kristjánsson (S164248)
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
When we sleep, it often looks like the body is just resting. However, the brain is working, the heart is
beating, and the kidneys are filtering the blood. We spend around 1/3 of our life sleeping [1]; therefore,
something must be happening. Furthermore, recent studies suggest that our sleep architecture is pre-
dictive for our survival. In 2020 Leary et al. [2] suggested that the amount of REM sleep was the best
predictor of mortality based on the four sleep stages (REM, N1, N2, N3). In an extension of the work
by Leary et al, the focus of this thesis was to include more sleep features to expand the knowledge of
accurate predictors for mortality.
My analysis included 657 features from the temporal and spectral domain of the Polysomnography
(PSG) data. PSG is the most common method of measuring sleep, giving information about the brain
(EEG), eyes (EOG), chin movement (EMG), and much more. To evaluate the frequency contents of
Electroencephalography (EEG), Electromyography (EMG), and Electroocoulogram (EOG) in the best
manner, a deep survival model was set up to analyze which frequency bands are predictive of survival.
The deep survival model was analyzed using the SHapley Additive exPlanations (SHAP) framework, a
game-theory approach to feature importance allowing us to bin the spectrum’s into relevant frequency
bands. The binned frequencies and the temporal features were then analyzed using Cox Proportional
Hazards model (Cox), facilitating the interpretation of all features in relation to survival analysis. The
investigation showed that a total of 132 features affected the mortality. The project showed promising
results and unveiled a multitude of possible further research, that might drastically change the way we
consider sleep and mortality.

==PAGE:10==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Knowing the length of one’s life has long been a subject of great interest for humanity. Within medical
science, the knowledge of patients remaining time alive, may allow for advantages such as life-extending
countermeasures and earlier/more precise diagnoses. Although much knowledge of remaining time of
survival has already been accumulated for various diseases, the evaluation of individuals without current
life-decreasing diagnoses remains a relatively unexplored area. For this reason, an investigation of sleep
and survival time has been conducted in the following thesis, aiming to uncover which characteristics
of the sleeping human body are relevant for determining survival time.
The project addresses a progressive and increasingly important subject within the area of medical
science. By analyzing and developing a capability to compare and evaluate the survival time of individ-
uals, this project enables further investigation and progress in a scientific field of great future potential
for the world of medical science. The investigation is conducted through a combination of frequency
and time dependent features of polysomnography data - such as heart rate, eye movement, and select
power bands. To execute the data processing, deep learning was used as a tool to uncover the relevance
of the different frequency bands. The Cox model was used to evaluate both the temporal and frequency
bands across sleep stages in relation survival time.
==PAGE:11==
1 Introduction
2
Problem Statement
This project aims to illuminate which characteristics of sleep data content are indicative for our survival.
Recently, Leary et al.[2] found that a decrease in REM sleep amount was the highest indicator of
mortality from the four sleep stages (REM, N1, N2, N3). This raises the question - which attributes
does the sleep stage have that are indicators of survival? The current procedure for evaluating which
features are important for survival is using the Cox Proportional Hazards Model. Although, researchers
are increasingly using deep learning methods to estimate survival. However, these deep learning survival
models often result in little to no ability to explain the results and focus primarily on achieving high
accuracy. The following project goals and statements are set to achieve a general understanding of the
important characteristics of sleep.
Project Goals
Main Goal: To understand which sleep data characteristics are important for survival using nocturnal
polysomnography data.
Aim 1: Develop a method to interpret the frequency content of EEG, EOG, and EMG to estimate
survival.
a) - Build, train, and test a deep learning model based on survival analysis.
b) - Apply feature importance analysis on deep learning results to understand the importance
of each feature in predicting the survival of individuals.
Aim 2: Extract temporal characteristics from the polysomnography data to investigate the
survival of patients.
Aim 3: Evaluate which sleep characteristics have the largest impact on survival
==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 2
Clinical Background
We spend about one third of our life sleeping [1]. Newer studies show that inadequate sleep is highly
related to disorders such as; hypertension, obesity, diabetes, cardiovascular diseases, mood disorders,
dementia and loneliness [3]. But what is happening when we sleep? This chapter goes behind the
human skull to explain the essential concepts of sleep.
2.1
Sleep Basics
Since 2005, sleep has been more and more in focus [3], and Google reports that searches of ’sleep’ has
tripled since 2004 [4]. The increasing amount of research in sleep has deepened the understanding of
the healthy brain remarkably. Today, sleep is typically investigated using polysomnography, from which
sleep characteristics can be extracted and analyzed when evaluating a person’s sleep.
2.1.1
Polysomnography
Polysomnography (PSG) is a set of physiological measurements performed usually on sleeping patients.
The complete set of a standard PSG can be seen in Figure 2.1. The PSG gives information regarding the
brain from the EEG, the eyes (EOG), the chin muscle tone (EMG), airflow, heart (Electrocardiogram
(ECG)), oxygen concentration (Peripheral Capillary Oxygen Saturation (SpO2)), respiratory effort
(Thoric and Abdominal belts), snoring, and finally the body position.
While some studies include
additional sensors, a typical PSG consists of the above-mentioned measurements[5].
Currently, the
practical use of PSG allows the medical personnel to accurately diagnose diseases such as; sleep apnea,
narcolepsy, REM sleep behaviour, and chronic insomnia.
Thus, the PSG provides a versatile and
clinically valuable range of information[5].
==PAGE:13==
2.1 Sleep Basics
4
Figure 2.1: An illustration of electrode placements and modalities recorded with when referring to a
PSG. It consist of EEG (multiple), EOG (left and right), EMG, Airflow, ECG, PPG, respiratory effort,
and body position. The figure is from [5]
2.1.2
American Academy of Sleep Medicine
From the PSG, the American Academy of Sleep Medicine (AASM) has defined five sleep stages that can
arise throughout the night. These stages are; Wake, Rapid Eye Movement (REM) sleep, Non-Rapid Eye
Movement Stage 1 (N1), Non-Rapid Eye Movement Stage 2 (N2), and Non-Rapid Eye Movement Stage
3 (N3) - with the last three often grouped as Non-Rapid Eye Movement (NREM). The classification
of each sleep stage is primarily defined by the frequency content of the EEG, but also includes eye
movements and chin EMG. The EEG frequencies are usually split into five bins: Delta: (0 - 3.99 Hz),
Theta: (4 - 7.99 Hz), Alpha: (8 - 12.99 Hz), Beta: (13 - 29.99 Hz), Gamma: (≥30 Hz1).
The scoring for each sleep stage is quite complex, but a simplified version is stated here for each of
the five sleep stages [6]:
Wake: Dominated by trains of Alpha activity, eyes blinking, irregular sharply peaked eye move-
ments and slow eye movements when the eyes are closed.
REM: Dominated by rapid eye movements, low EMG activity, sawtooth waves (2-6 Hz) preceding
a burst of rapid eye movements, and finally transient muscle activity.
N1: This sleep stage is often the first sleep stage after sleep onset. It is defined by Theta activity,
and slow eye movements.
N2: The start of N2 is defined by either K-complexes or sleep spindles. K-complexes are charac-
terized by an abrupt negativity in the EEG, followed by a longer lasting positivity [7]. The sleep
spindles are frequencies of 11-16 Hz lasting more than 0.5 seconds.
N3: Dominant frequency of 0.5 - 2 Hz and a peak-to-peak amplitude of >75 µV from the EEG.
1Gamma is no longer considered by AASM, but is widely used within research. AASM considers Betas range as being
greater than 13 Hz
==PAGE:14==
2.1 Sleep Basics
5
Figure Figure 2.2 illustrates the differences between sleep stages in the modalities EEG, EOG, and
EMG.
The sleep stages usually arise in the following order: N1, N2, N3, N2, and finally REM. This is
called one sleep cycle, and there are commonly 4 to 5 cycles during a night’s sleep[9].
2.1.3
Age effect on sleep
Many factors change the content of the PSG - age, gender, diseases, and medications, just to mention
a few. One of the fundamental changes is seen in the sleep architecture. With increasing age, the
proportion of REM and N3 sleep are decreased, and the proportion of N1 and N2 increases. Furthermore,
a recent study by Leary et al. [2] showed that decreased REM sleep increases mortality, independently
of age. The EEG content also changes with gender and age. The frequencies and the power of the EEG
generally shift towards higher frequencies as we get older [10], and women generally have higher EEG
power than men [10]. All of these factors have to be taken into consideration when modelling mortality
based on Polysomnography data.
==PAGE:15==
2.1 Sleep Basics
6
Figure 2.2: A figure comparing the sleep stages for the modalitites EEG, EOG, and EMG. a) shows
a hypnogram for a patient, where one colored point is selected for each sleep stage. The colors correlate
to the sleep stages in b), and c) indicates the axis used. Reprint from [8] with permission from Andreas
Brink-Kjær.
==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER 3
State-of-the-Art
3.1
Search process
The method for selecting state-of-the-art papers was based on the findit.dtu.dk [11] website. The
analysis was performed in March 2022.
An overall visualization of the search process can be seen in Figure 3.1. The initial search was; ”Sur-
vival Analysis,” which resulted in 48568 results. Since only the current state-of-the-art is of interest,
a number of additional requirements were set. Only English journal articles from no later than 2020.
This reduced the number of articles down to 9898. If adding the keyword; ”EEG”, six results show up.
However, none of them are investigating the EEG power spectrum. Meaning no survival analysis has
been performed on the EEG power spectrum. When changing the date to 2014, there are 24 articles;
however, none evaluate the EEG power spectrum in relation to mortality. When looking up state of
the art for methodology of survival analysis, the keywords; ”Survival Analysis” + ”Deep learning” were
used. This resulted in 78 papers. The selection of papers was based on citations and an Altmetric score.
In the next section, a summary of 10 articles are selected. The first is out of a state-of-the-art search
criterion but is highly relevant for the entire project. Two did not have the highest number of citations,
but after reading them, they had suitable methodologies that adapted some of the state-of-the-art arti-
cles’ problems.
After the condensation of articles, there is a summary highlighting what is currently being done in
survival analysis and the current problems within the area.
It should be noted that this section was written prior to any analysis made, and thus before any
considerations of limitations and feasibility.
==PAGE:17==
3.2 Condensation of articles
8
Figure 3.1: The search process to find state-of-the-art articles. starting with findit.dtu.dk, and ending
in 9 selected articles. The quotation marks are included in the search term, and the top 9 were selected
based on citations and Altmetric score
3.2
Condensation of articles
Association of Rapid Eye Movement Sleep With Mortality in Middle-aged and Older Adults
Eileen B. Leary, Kathleen T. Watson, Sonia Ancoli-Israel, Susan Redline, Kristine Yaffe, MD, Laurel
A. Ravelo, Paul E. Peppard, James Zou, Steven N. Goodman, Emmanuel Mignot, and Katie L. Stone
This study investigates which of the four sleep stages are of the highest importance in relation to
mortality [2].
Data Description: The data used is from Outcomes of Sleep Disorders in Older Men (MrOs) and
Wisconsin Sleep Cohort (WSC), including 2675 and 1386 individuals, respectively. MrOs only contains
men with a mean age of 76.3 (SD of 5.5) years who participated in the study for an average of 12.1
years. WSC included men and women of age 51.5 (SD of 8.5), and were included for 20.8 years on
average.
Method: From polysomnography(PSG), the time spent in the four sleep stages, N1, N2, N3,
and REM were extracted. The study primarily used Cox proportional hazards model to assess the
association between all-cause, cardiovascular, cancer, and other mortality. The primary evaluation was
made on MrOs, with the WSC dataset used for validation. Several confounders were included, such as
actigraphy, self-recorded sleep assessment data, mental, and physical measures. Leary et al. tried to
match as many covariates as available in both cohorts. Methods such as Random survival forest and
==PAGE:18==
3.2 Condensation of articles
9
Kaplan-Mayer estimator were used for variable importance test and to split the data into appropriate
groups.
Results: The results showed that every 5% decrease in REM sleep increased mortality by 13%
and 17% for MrOs and WSC, respectively.
The results yielded similar results when adjusting for
demographics, sleep, and health covariates. Individuals with more than 15% REM sleep had between
20-35 % lower risk of mortality than those with less than 15% REM sleep. A random forest model,
furthermore, identified the percentage of REM sleep as the most crucial sleep stage concerning mortality.
My Comments: Eileen B. Leary later released a conference paper, [12], including the Sleep Heart
Health Study (SHHS), which showed the same trend. This raised speculations of what aspects within
REM sleep are relevant for increased mortality. Furthermore, the analysis did not include the percentage
of time spent scored as awake, which could also be highly important in relation to mortality.
Deep learning survival analysis for multiple brain metastases
Enoch Chang, Marina Joel, Hannah Chang, Justin Du, James Yu, Yi An, James Hansen, Antonio
Omuro, Veronica Chiang, and Sanjay Aneja
The purpose of this study is to use deep learning survival models on MRI images post-primary
stereotactic radiosurgery to predict the survival of patients [13].
Data description: 831 patients were treated using primary stereotactic radiosurgery at the De-
partment of Therapeutic Radiology at Yale School of Medicine from 2000-2018 - where a total of 3596
brain metastases were treated.
Method: From MRI images, 851 3D radiomic features were extracted using T1 weighted images for
brain metastasis. The 851 features were reduced using the minimum redundancy maximum relevance
test. DeepSurv was used to train the model - a Cox proportional hazard neural network architecture
in Python and R. The traditional method is the disease-specific Graded Prognostic Assessment (GPA),
which was used as a reference parameter here. The GPA includes age, Karnofsky Performance Score,
extra-cranial metastases, molecular profile, and the number of metastases [14]. To assess the perfor-
mance of the two models, the concordance indices were used using 100 bootstrapped samples of 415
patients with a 2-sample t-test to check if there was a statistical difference.
Results: The deep learning algorithm had a concordance index of 84.8% (95% CI [81.1%, 87.7%]).
The widely used GPA had a concordance index of only 38% (95% CI [35.6%, 40.3%]), where the 2-sample
t-test showed a significant improvement with p<0.001.
My Comments: The analysis proves that extracting features from a signal, which in this case is
an image, can increase the predicting accuracy significantly. However, this analysis is quite the black
box, and it would be interesting to analyze which specific features are important when estimating brain
cancer patients’ survival.
Coronavirus disease 2019 (COVID‑19): survival analysis using deep learning and Cox
regression model
Mostafa Atlam, Hanaa Torkey, Nawal El-Fishawy, and Hanaa Salem
This paper aims to help medical staff select which COVID-19 patients to focus on by using survival
analysis. The study uses an autoencoder deep neural network in combination with Cox regression to
predict the survival probability based on many features. Furthermore, it tries to find which features
are most important for survival [15].
==PAGE:19==
3.2 Condensation of articles
10
Data description: The study contains 1085 patients who participated in the study. The data
contains several features, including demography (age, gender, etc.), common symptoms (fever, cough,
pneumonia, etc.), and other symptoms (chills, joint pain, diarrhea, etc.). Every patient is labeled for
how long they have participated in the study and if they are alive or not. 576 patients were removed
due to insuﬀicient data, leaving 509 patients for the analysis.
Method: An autoencoder was used as an unsupervised artificial neural network. This was primarily
used to remove noise from the data - as good data produces good results [16]. The autoencoder consisted
of; Encoder, Bottleneck, Decoder, and Reconstruction.
They proposed two methods of predicting
survival probability. Model 1 applies the autoencoder and then the cox regression, whereas Model 2
updates the weights in the autoencoder by using 100 epochs, a batch size of 16, and a validation split
of 45%.
Results: A threshold was used to assess the performance. Patients with a probability of survival
above the threshold are more likely to survive, whereas people below the same threshold are more likely
to die. When using this method, model 1 obtains 95.71% test accuracy, whereas model 2 obtains 95.71%
test accuracy. The authors models are compared to other methods of predicting survival. The other
models have the highest accuracy of 71.47%, indicating that this papers model is vastly superior.
My Comments: The entire process of this paper is good. Improve model performance, and then
figure out which characteristics are of high importance in relation to COVID-19 deaths. However, their
method of evaluation is not compare-able to the other models, and this skew the comparison highly.
Deep learning based feature-level integration of multi-omics data for breast cancer patients
survival analysis
Li Tong, Jonathan Mitchel, Kevin Chatlin, and May D. Wang
This paper aims to find a personalized diagnosis and treatment for breast cancer patients. The first
step is to develop an algorithm with high accuracy using survival analysis - which is the focus of this
paper.
Data description: Two types of data are used in this study’s dataset. First, the MNIST database
used 60.000 training samples and 10.000 testing samples for testing the model using the TCGA-BRCA
breast cancer multi-omics database. 1060 patients were used with gene expression, miRNA expression,
DNA methylation, and CNV data - including survival information. The MNIST dataset had its images
flipped and added with either Gaussian noise or random erasing to simulate other modalities. The multi-
omics dataset is pre-processed, by removing missing data, min-max normalizing following a Four-Fold
Cross-Validation
Methods: Three methods were tested. All are related to autoencoders, which consists of input
features, hidden features, and reconstructed features. In the simplest model, a single omics dataset was
trained and tested on a single autoencoder. The hidden features were fed into a fully connected neural
network, using the negative log partial likelihood loss function (inspired by the Cox proportional hazards
model). For the second model (ConcatAE), two modalities were introduced to two autoencoders. The
hidden features from each autoencoder were concatenated into one feature vector, which was then fed
into a feed-forward network of a similar structure. In the last model (CrossAE), two autoencoders were
used with two modalities. However, the network was trained, and the hidden feature decoder for both
autoencoders was used on both hidden features. Additionally, an element-wise average of the hidden
features of both autoencoders was then sent to the survival feed-forward neural network.
==PAGE:20==
3.2 Condensation of articles
11
Results: Testing using the MNIST dataset, unveiled that the ConcatAE had the best performance.
When using random erasing as the second modality resulted in an accuracy of 96.2%, 96.3% precision,
and 96.2% recall. However, when using the Gaussian noise, the CrossAE had the best performance
of 92.3% Accuracy, 93.3% Precision, and 93.3% recall.
Furthermore, using an -omics dataset, the
ConcatAE has the highest performance of 64.1%, with use PCA to reduce the number of features and
using the DnaMeth + miRNA as the two modalities.
My Comments: The performance was of decent quality and certainly improved using this multi-
omics method combining the autoencoders. However, it is quite the black box. There is no information
regarding which parts of the -omics dataset are important. Which genes are of the highest importance?
Even though the performance was decent, without any interpretation there is limited knowledge to gain
from this method.
Deep Learning-Based Survival Analysis for High-Dimensional Survival Data
Lin Hao, Juncheol Kim, Sookhee Kwon, and Il Do Ha
The purpose of this paper was to develop a simple deep neural network for survival analysis, and
compare it to other traditional networks used within survival analysis. It focuses on high-dimensional
data, where there are more features than observations [17].
Data description: The paper used three datasets; EMTAB386 (A), GSE49997(B) and TCGAMirna
(C). Dataset A contains mRNA and microRNA information on ovarian cancers and has 129 observa-
tions, with 10357 covariates and a censoring rate of 43.4%. B has gene features of 194 ovarian cancer
patients with a total of 16048 covariates and a 70.6% censoring rate. Dataset C contains data from
554 high-grade serous ovarian cancer patients with 799 gene features and a 47.4% censoring rate. The
datasets all included clinical variables, which were also included in some of the models. Each patient
was labeled with whether or not they were censored and the time to event. The data was, furthermore,
pre-processed, removing all missing values and columns with the same parameters, and continuous
variables were normalized.
Methods: One deep-learning model (DNNSurv) was tested against three otherwise known survival
models (Random Survival Forest (RSF), Cox-Lasso, Cox-Ridge). The DNNSurv consisted of one input
layer, two hidden layers, and one output layer. The model uses partial log-likelihood as a loss function
with an L1 penalty and a SeLU as the activation function. For evaluation, all models used 10-fold
cross-validation. For hyper-parameter tuning of the DNNSurv, a 10-fold cross-validation grid search
was used.
Results: The C-index, time-dependent Brier score, and time-dependent AUC were all used to
evaluate the performance of the different models.
The DNNSurv outperforms the other models in
almost every category and every evaluation method. For the C-index, there is not a single model that
beats DNNSurv. For evaluation at 5 years for the Brier score, Cox-Lasso and Cox-Ridge marginally
outperform the DNNSurv on only dataset A, not including clinical data. And for the 5-year AUC, the
DNNSurv performs every other model on all parameters, suggesting that the Deep learning network
is superior to the traditional machine learning approaches.
However, the DNNSurv took about 50
minutes to run, whereas the other models took 44.1 seconds to run combined, and the performance of
the DNNSurv model was highly sensitive to the chosen hyper-parameters.
My Comments: Interesting and quite simple deep learning approach. It would likely be possible
to increase the performance by increasing the complexity of the network structure. However, Lin Hao et
al. seem to have evaluation speed in mind, which explains the simplicity of the network. It is interesting
that the Random Survival Forest has the worst performance, as it is highly used in literature elsewhere.
==PAGE:21==
3.2 Condensation of articles
12
Lung cancer survival period prediction and understanding: Deep learning approaches
Shreyesh Doppalapudi, Robin G. Qiu, and Youakim Badr
This paper focuses on the survival analysis of lung cancer patients using different survival predictor
models. However, the paper also focuses on feature importance to see what increases or decreases the
risk of death, both on an individual basis and generally for the entire population [18].
Data description: The data used is from the Surveillance, Epidemiology and End Results (SEER)
program. This dataset contains cancer data from 34.6% of the U.S. population. The data contains
demographics, relevant tumor information (stage, site, morphology, etc.), vital status, and much more.
The pre-processing included removing variables with more than 20% missing data, while the rest of the
missing data was imputed using the median value for quantitative variables and mode for categorical.
One-hot encoding was, furthermore, performed on the categorical variables.
Methods: For the classification approach, the target is changed to ≤6 months, 0.5-2 years, and
2 years, whereas for the regression, the evaluation is kept in months. The dataset was normalized, as it
helps the model avoid local minimums. It was also split with a training and test ratio of 80/20. Three
deep learning architectures and three machine learning classification models were selected; ANN, RNN,
and CNN for deep learning, and; Random Forest, SVM, and Naïves Bayes Class for classification. Four
regression models were tested: Random Forest Regressor, Gradient Boosting Machine, Line Regression
Model, and Custom Ensemble model. Accuracy, Precision, Recall, F-1 score, Cohen’s K, and Area
Under ROC curve (AUC) were used to evaluate the classification models. MSE, RMSE, and Coeﬀicient
Determination (R2) were used as evaluation metrics for the regression models. To interpret the results,
SHapley Addictive exPlanations (SHAP) was used for feature importance on the output on the training
set.
Results: Considering the classification analysis, the deep learning models outperform the ma-
chine learning approaches. ANN had the highest accuracy of 71.17; however, it is close between the
RNN(70.34%) and CNN (70.34%). The regression analysis has the same tendency where the deep learn-
ing methods are significantly better than the machine learning methods. The CNN has the highest R2
of 50.66%, with RNN as a close contender with R2 of 50.09% and ANN of 47.78%. None of the other
models had a R2 of 41%. It turned out that the RMSE increased as the number of months increased,
because the number of people alive decreased.
My Comments: They have a lot of data, and there are no censored patients. There is no traditional
use of survival analysis (Cox-PH, Kaplan-Maier, Cox-Ridge, Cox-LASSO). Because of the high RMSE
over time, removing the patients who survived for longer than i.e. 4000 days might have made sense.
Prediction of conversion to Alzheimer’s disease using deep survival analysis of MRI images
Tomonori Nakagawa, Manabu Ishida, Junpei Naito, Atsushi Nagai, Shuhei Yamaguchi, and Keiichi
Onoda
This paper aims to use survival analysis to predict the risk of patients developing Alzheimer’s disease
(AD) using deep learning approaches on MRI imaging data [19].
Data description: Four cohorts were combined for this analysis, which resulted in a total of
2142 patients. Among the patients were people with mild cognitive impairment and controls. The
data contained MRIs and additional other medical information regarding the patients. With the goal
of splitting the image into grey matter, white matter, and cerebrospinal fluid, the images were pre-
processed. A Grey Matter Volume (GMV) was extracted based on the Brainnetome atlas and automated
==PAGE:22==
3.2 Condensation of articles
13
anatomical labeling. The data had 116 covariates, with labels on whether they developed Alzheimer’s
and number of days since the MRI analysis.
Methods: Nakagawa et al. made their own deep survival model architecture. The model con-
sisted of an input layer with three hidden layers with a ReLU activation function. It uses L1 and L2
penalization in each layer. The fifth layer consisted of two single units fed into a cumulative Weibull
distribution, resulting in a probability of survival. They produced their loss function, which considers
the censoring of patients. When patients are censored, the loss becomes zero at the time after censoring
- as a result of this censoring bias is removed. The dataset was split 80% for training, 10% for validation,
and 10% for testing. The model was compared to the regular Cox Proportional Hazards model (Cox)
and another deep learning framework; DeepHit. DeepHit is also based on the Cox model, but differs as
it takes competing risks into account.
Results: To evaluate the models, the concordance index was used. Their model produces slightly
better performance results than the DeepHit Model but significantly better than the regular Cox model.
The paper gradually increased the complexity of the input, which highly increased the model’s perfor-
mance. The analysis was also performed on only MCI patients without controls, which resulted in a
lower c-index.
My Comments: Next step would be to figure out which brain segments are of significance to
survival. Maybe it is the general loss of gray matter, maybe it is an increase in grey matter, or it might
be dependent on specific zones of the brain. All of the aforementioned, could lead to very interesting
future analysis. This study only considered Alzheimer’s; however, patients with another dementia form
probably highly confounded the data, decreasing the accuracy.
Automated CT biomarkers for opportunistic prediction of future cardiovascular events and
mortality in an asymptomatic screening population: a retrospective cohort study
Perry J. Pickhardt, Peter M. Graffy, Ryan Zea, Scott J. Lee, Jiamin Liu, Veit Sandfort, and Ronald
M. Summers
This paper uses CT images and traditional survival analysis methods to evaluate feature importance
[20].
Data description: 9223 healthy and asymptomatic patients undergoing a CT scan for colorectal
cancer screening were included in the study. The study uses deep learning to process the CT images,
which extracts relevant features, such as segmentation and quantification of the spine, aortic calcium,
abdominal musculature, visceral and subcutaneous fat, and liver. The scope of this paper was not to
produce these features, but to use the features for survival analysis.. Furthermore, the data contained
age, sex, and other clinical parameters. The ’event’ parameter was selected when a patient died or had
a major cardiovascular event after the CT scan.
Methods: When the features had been extracted using deep learning methods, the traditional Cox
proportional hazards model was used to evaluate the features. For this, two evaluation metrics were
used; Area under the receiver operating characteristic curves (AUROCs) and concordance index from
the Cox model. For the AUROC the analysis was split into patients surviving at least 2,5 or 10 years,
determined by the follow-up time. The analysis was performed individually on the five features and
BMI and Framingham Risk Score. The analysis later included a multivariate analysis, where a selection
of combinations was performed to increase the performance.
Results: The highest concordance index of the univariate features proved to be the aortic calcifi-
cation with a c-index of 0.735. The multivariate analysis all contained the aortic calcification feature
==PAGE:23==
3.2 Condensation of articles
14
and had the highest concordance index when using a combination of muscle (Hounsfield units) + liver
(Hounsfield units) + visceral to subcutaneous fat ratio, resulting in a c-index of 0.778. The same ten-
dency was seen for 2-, 5- and 10-year-AUROC scores. Kaplan-Maier plots were used to visualize all
features. The plots add to the significance of the ’extremes’, and their effect on mortality.
My Comments: The approach is straightforward. Furthermore, there is a consensus that too high
a fat ratio all over the body has negative effects. There is no particular reason to use deep learning
approaches, because there is no expected non-linearity and only few features.
Time-to-event prediction using survival analysis methods for Alzheimer’s disease progression
Rahul Sharma, Harsh Anand, Youakim Badr, and Robin G. Qiu
This paper aims to categorize a worsening in Alzheimer’s disease and evaluate a deep learning model
(N-MTLR) against the Cox proportional hazards deep learning model to overcome some limitations [21].
Data description: The paper uses data from >36000 patients with multiple visits, resulting in
a total of 123417 records and 92 features. These features contain demographics, medications, health
history, physical parameters, and disease metrics. Some similar features were removed using a random
forest, and the rest of the data was imputed using the mean for continuous and mode for categorical
data. Feature selection was implemented to reduce the number of features to 50. However, the same
analysis was also completed, including all (79) features.
Methods: Two network architectures were built for a CoxPH deep learning model and a Neural
multi-task logistic regression model, including 1 and 4 layers. The evaluation is based on the C-index
and the Integrated Brier Score (IBS). The data was split into 64%, 16%, 20% for training, validation,
and testing, respectively.
Results: The two models have very similar c-index scores. The Cox Model has a high IBS score
when all features are included.
Otherwise, there is close to no performance difference between the
models. The highest concordance index belongs to N-MTLR with four layers, while making use of only
50 features (concordance index of 0.79). The analysis does, however, show that decreasing the number
of features for this network can increase the model.
My Comments: The model performances are close to the same. The conclusion that N-MTLR
model performs better should be reevaluated. Running the other models multiple times might result in
equally good results. Some variables, such as N-MTLR loss, are not explained, nor to be found in the
referenced literature.
Survival Analysis Using Surgeon Skill Metrics and Patient Factors to Predict Urinary
Continence Recovery After Robot-assisted Radical Prostatectomy
Loc Trinh and Samuel Mingo and Erik B. Vanstrum and Daniel I. Sanford and Aastha and Runzhuo
Ma and Jessica H. Nguyen and Yan Liu, and Andrew J. Hung
This paper tries to predict urinary continence recovery using the survival analysis approach. It
makes use of data related to the surgeon’s skills and patient-relevant features [22].
Data description: Data from robot-assisted radical prostatectomy procedures was collected from
115 patients. Several features related to the skill-set of the surgeons that are used when they undergo
training were used. The model also includes preoperative data of the patient, baseline characteristics, etc.
The ’event’ in this case was defined as the time patients stopped wearing pads following the operation.
==PAGE:24==
3.3 Summary of articles
15
The operators were rated by trainers based on videos of the surgeries. These ratings included needle
positioning, needle entry, tissue trauma, suture placement, tissue approximation, and knot tying. All
missing data were imputed by using the median value.
Methods: This paper used the traditional Cox proportional hazard model to evaluate the survival
probability. Furthermore, they used DeepSurv; a deep learning architecture using the Cox Proportional
Hazard as a loss function, where the network contains two hidden layers. The evaluation of the models
is the c-index. Two analysis methods were performed: The full dataset and a set number of features.
The feature selection was based on the change in the concordance, when randomly permuting a variable.
Results: When combining all features, the DeepSurv model proved to have the highest c-index
of 0.783, when only looking at a selected number of features. However, the regular Cox model with
selected parameters is the second highest with 0.713. A series of features were additionally tested, where
the DeepSurv model had the highest c-index over all models. The features of highest importance were
needle positioning, master clutch usage, and age. The results show that the quality of the surgeon is
essential- suggesting that more training is needed to reduce mistakes.
My Comments: The feature importance test is based on the change in concordance index, when
one feature is permuted. This limits the actual importance as it does not take interactions into account.
However, inspecting which features are relevant for the model performance makes the model useful for
medical professionals.
3.3
Summary of articles
The articles investigated in this chapter derive from various scientific fields, although their methodologies
are quite similar. The methodological approaches of the articles [13, 15, 23, 17, 18, 19, 22] are based
on building a deep learning survival model and compare it to the performance of the traditional Cox
analysis, which is widely used today. The papers use a variety of different approaches. Some use the
DeepSurv architecture [13, 22], which uses the Cox loss function, while others make an FNN [18, 21,
19], CNN [18], or RNN [18]. Finally, some use DeepHit [19]; an architecture that takes the competing
risks of the features into account.
There is a general consensus that the deep learning approaches
perform better. Despite researching existing scientific sources, no research supporting the claim that
the traditional methods are better, or equal to, the deep learning approaches. That said, one must
consider the high degree of confirmation bias on this area, as there is a tendency for such results not to
be published. Many of the models are black box models. Some of them use random survival forest to
check feature importance, but this unveils nothing about the black box model which achieved the high
accuracy. [22] uses a permutation of single features to investigate whether the concordance changes,
but this is a sub-optimal solution, as it does not consider interaction terms. S. Doppalapudi [18] relies
upon a more sustainable approach, using game-theory, SHAP, to detect feature importance. However,
S. Doppalapudi did not have censored patients, making the deep learning approach different than for
the other articles. This means that despite the fact that a good performance can be achieved by using
deep learning survival models, it is not very useful, because of its low degree of interpretability. In the
initial search, there is no literature regarding survival analysis on the power spectrum of EEGs
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 4
Background
This chapter takes the reader through the mathematical background needed to understand the project.
First, a general understanding of survival analysis is given, then the Cox Proportional Hazards Model
is explained, including censoring, the loss function, and evaluation method (Concordance index). Later
a general understanding of neural networks is given, explaining feed-forward neural networks, convo-
lutional neural networks, activation functions, and stochastic gradient descent. Lastly, an explanation
of the collaborative game-theory approach to feature importance framework, SHAP is given. If these
concepts are well known, then these sections can be skipped.
4.1
Survival analysis
Survival analysis is a statistical method of assessing the expected duration until a time event occurs. It
is widely used within the medical field to estimate survival between patient groups but is also used to
detect failure time for machines or time till unemployment. Unique to this analysis is that the event
(e.g. death) has not occurred for all patients. These patients could typically not be used within regular
models as their path to the desired outcome is not empirically known, and they are therefore referred
to as censored. This has been visualized in Figure 4.1. The figure shows the concept of right-censoring.
There are two types of right-censoring, one of which is when a person does not want to participate in
the study anymore, like patient C, and the other is observed data without the occurrence of an event
like patients D and E. Patients A, B, and F are examples of patients who experienced an event at the
time, 3, 4 and 4, respectively. Censoring essentially refers to data from subjects in which no time event
has occurred.
In medical literature, three methods are commonly used to handle censored data; Kaplan-Meier
Figure 4.1:
Figure illustrating censoring.
The arrows illustrate patients.
The start of the arrow
indicates when they first participated in the study. The arrowhead indicates when they either died
(brown) or were censored (green).
==PAGE:26==
4.1 Survival analysis
17
estimator (KM), log-rank test, and Cox Proportional Hazards model (Cox). The Cox model has been
selected to be the focus of this project.
4.1.1
Cox Proportional Hazards Model
In 1972 D.R. Cox formulated a way of estimating the Hazard Ratio (HR) between two groups of people
[24]. He utilized the hazard function, which estimates the chances of a patient dying exactly at time t.
The hazard function that D.R. Cox was utilizing is given by:
h(t) = h0(t) · eβ1x1+β2x2+...+βkxk,
(4.1)
where t is time, h(t) is the hazard function, βk is the impact of the covariate xk, and h0 is the baseline
hazard. D.R. Cox had the idea of comparing two groups of people. Let us take an example where
smokers, Xs and non-smokers, Xn are compared. The hazard function for the two groups is:
hs(t) = h0(t) · eβs1xs1
hn(t) = h0(t) · eβn1xn1
(4.2)
The Hazard Ratio (HR) is simply the hazard functions divided by each other:
HR = hs(t)
hn(t) = h0(t) · eβs1xs1
h0(t) · eβn1xn1 = eβs1xs1
eβn1xn1
(4.3)
The hazard ratio thereby eliminates the baseline hazard, meaning that the baseline hazard is assumed
to be constant over time between the two groups. Let us suppose three scenarios:
• HR > 1 : Smokers have an increased risk of dying
• HR = 1 : No difference between groups
• HR < 1 : Smoking is protective
Let us suppose that the hazard ratio is 2. This means that the likelihood of dying as a smoker at
any time compared to non-smokers is 100% higher [25].
But how do we find the coeﬀicients for the covariates included in the model? This can be done by
computing a partial likelihood.
Likelihood functions are typically written as the likelihood for the output of a function based on the
parameters of all observations across an entire sample group, whereas the Partial Likelihood (PL) is
the product of the likelihoods for each of the observed participants within the group. If J is the number
of events, then PL can be described as:
PL =
J
Y
j=1
Lj
(4.4)
Imagine that we have a set of 100 patients, which are ordered so patient 1 was in the study for the
shortest time, and patient 100 the longest.
For every patient, the question asked is: what is the probability that you died, compared to the
ones who lived longer. The answer for patient 1 can be expressed by the following formula:
L1 =
h1(t1)
h1(t1) + h2(t1) + · · · + h100(t1)
(4.5)
==PAGE:27==

18
And for patient 2:
L2 =
h2(t2)
h2(t2) + h3(t2) + · · · + h100(t2)
(4.6)
Meaning: the hazard of patient i at time ti, divided by the sum of hazards for those who lived longer
than ti.
The set of people in the denominator is often referred to as the risk set.
This has just been a generalization of L for all patients. If we insert the hazard function, it shows
the following expression for patient 2:
L2 =
h0(t2)eβx2

(4.7)
As the baseline hazard is included in all terms, the likelihood for patient 2 is reduced to:
L2 =

(4.8)

where Yij = 1 if tj ≥ti and Yij = 0 if tj < ti. Where Yij is binary, indicating whether or not it is
included in the risk set.
However, if two patients are recorded to die or are censored at the same time, we say that they are
tied at events and the formula does not hold. The way this project handles this is by using Efron’s
approximation [26].

The log partial likelihood is optimized over β’s for every feature in the vector xk.
In this project the covariates investigated will primarily be continuous. To better understand the
results, the features are standardized using the following formula:
z = X −µ
σ
(4.16)
X being the feature vector for every patient, µ the mean, and σ the standard deviation. Standardizing
the features to the same scale allows us to directly compare the βs and thus makes the results inter-
pretable.
After successfully minimizing the ln(PL), we can test the null hypothesis that β is 0 (or eβ = 1)
under a 95% confidence interval.
If the test result gives a result of eβ = 1.2 with p-val < 0.05, then the conclusion is for every one increase
in the standard deviation of the feature investigated, there is a 20% increased likelihood of dying at
any point. Whereas eβ = 0.8 with p-val < 0.05, shows that every one increase in standard deviation is
protective with 20%.
4.1.1.1
Concordance index
Concordance Index (CI) is a way of evaluating the performance of the model. The CI can be understood
as describing the following: If we compare every patient pairwise to each other, how often does the
model correctly predict which patient lives longer? Figure 4.2 contains a set of information such as
time, censoring, and their risk score as an output from the model.
The (Harrell’s) concordance index calculates every possible combination of permissible pairs and
checks if the risk assessment was correct. The person with a higher risk score should have a shorter
time to the event. However, not all patient pairs are permissible. Let us set T to the time in which
they either were censored or an event happened, and η as their risk score. There are five scenarios that
can happen when we are comparing a set of patients:
• If both experienced an event:
– Ti < Tj and ηi > ηj, (and reversed), These are a permissible pair, and are concordant.
– Ti < Tj and ηi < ηj, (and reversed). Permissible but not concordant.
• Both are censored - not permissible.
• Only one experiences event (Ti):
==PAGE:29==
4.1 Survival analysis
20
– If Tj < Ti then they are non-permissible.
– If Tj > Ti then the pair is permissible, and if ηi > ηj it is concordant.
An equation can be set up:
CI = Number of concordant pairs
Number of permissable pairs
(4.17)
This can be written as a formula using logical statements.
CI =
P
i̸=j 1 {ηi < ηj} 1 {Ti > Tj} dj
P
i̸=j 1 {Ti > Tj} dj
(4.18)
where dj is 0 if the patient was censored, otherwise 1.
Using this formula we can calculate the concordance index of the example in Figure 4.2.
The permissible pairs are:
Permissible={AC,AE,BC,BE,CD,CE}
(4.19)
Whereas the number of concordant pairs are:
Concordant = {AC, AE, CD}
(4.20)
Thereby calculated as:
CI = 3
6 = 0.5
(4.21)
A CI of 50% is no better than a coin-flip, 70% indicates a good model, and 100% means the model
could with 100% certainty predict who will experience an event before another.
Figure 4.2: This figure is a table including patients, their Risk Score, the time they have been in the
study, and if they are censored or not. Inspired by [28]
One of the perks of Cox Proportional Hazards model is the model can include confounders, which
makes it possible to adjust for, e.g. age and sex. This project investigates four models with increasing
amounts of confounders, see table Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: An overview of models and their confounders used in the project. The features marked
with bold are categorical features, and the others are continuous

By having multiple confounders we ensure that the model is not describing e.g. age using some
other feature, which then causes it to become significant.
4.2
Neural Networks
The Terminator Movie is an example of the horrors neural networks and Artificial Intelligence (AI)
can cause when out of control. Besides the fact that this is not a realistic scenario, the concept of a
computer making an intelligent decision by itself is not far away. Over the recent years, these intelligent
decisions have been increasingly made using neural networks. Neural networks are trained to perform
a specific task given a specific input. A task could be determining whether an image (visual input)
contains an animal, human, or object.
In this project, the focus is less on world domination and more on the survivability of a patient. This
section takes the reader through the mathematics of Feed Forward Neural Network (FFNN), Activation
functions, Loss functions, Stochastic Gradient Descent, Convolutional Neural Network (CNN), and Max
Pooling. If the reader has a general knowledge of neural networks and the aforementioned terms, then
this section may be skipped.
4.2.1
Feed Forward Neural Network
Figure 4.3 illustrates a simple Feed Forward Neural Network, which consists of D inputs, a set of hidden
layers, and k outputs, with their corresponding weight. The number of outputs is dependent on the
problem that is at hand [29]. If the purpose of the model is to determine if the image contains an animal,
human, or object, then there would be three output nodes. The output nodes each represent one of the
classes and contains the probability of being that class. There is one weight per connection of nodes,
denoted w. The subscript suggests which two nodes are connected. The superscript indicates which
layer the weight is emerging from. For instance, w(2)
12 describes the connection between Z2 and Y1, where
Z2 is the second node in the hidden layer, and Y1 is the first node in the output layer. The weights are
adjustable, and they are updated with each iteration of backpropagation. The model includes an offset
constant (bias), denoted as x0, z0. The bias constants are set to 1; however, the weights regarding the
bias constants are updated. In the learning process, all inputs to a node are summarized and passed
through an activation function, σ. The output from node z(1)
m can be calculated as:

4.2.2
Activation Function
Activation functions are non-linear functions which allow the network to model non-linear relationships
between the input and output [29]. Three common non-linear activation functions are visualized in Fig-
ure 4.4, Sigmoid, tanh, and Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU). The activation functions are mathematically
described as :
sigmoid(x) =
1
1 + e−x
tanh(x) =
2
1 + e−2x −1
ReLU(x) = max(0, x)
(4.26)
Where x is the input to the activation function. A problem when using the Sigmoid and tanh activation
function is vanishing gradients (see 4.2.4), which is not a problem with ReLU. In this project, the ReLU
function has therefore been selected.

[30]

4.2 Neural Networks

4.2.3
Loss Function
The loss function of a neural network is used to optimize the weights. The loss function should therefore
be specific to the problem at hand and describe the error of the network with respect to the task. The
loss function used in this project was inspired by the partial log-likelihood explained and derived in
Section 4.1.1. It was inspired by Lin Hao’s paper from 2021 [17]. One of the perks of using a deep learning
model instead of a linear model like Cox is that it can achieve complex non-linear interactions between
the features. In equation (4.15) the model instead tries to optimize an unknown function, g(x; w), where
x is the vector containing the covariates, and w the corresponding weights to be optimized. The log
partial likelihood used as the loss function is thereby described as:
ℓ(x; w) =

(4.27)
The input to the neural network is high-dimensional data, which increases the risk of the model
overfitting to the training dataset. Therefore, Lasso Regression (L1) regularization is implemented,
which applies a penalty to the loss function. The L1 regularization term can be written as:
L1 = λ
p
X
j=0
|wj| ,
(4.28)
where λ is a hyperparameter that must be carefully selected, p is the number of features, and wj is
the trainable weight for the jth feature. A λ of 0 means there is no regularization, and λ →∞leads
to extreme underfitting, since it forces the weigths toward zero. Therefore, it is necessary to tune this
parameter through multiple training iterations to find the optimal setting.
Combining the regularization term from equation (4.28) with the loss function from equation (4.27)
and placing a negative sign in front of it to turn it from a maximization to a minimization problem
yields the final loss function which was optimized by the neural network:

4.2.4
Stochastic Gradient Descent
The loss function only computes how far the output of the network is from the true output values for the
current set weights. However, the wonder of deep learning is the method it uses to update the weights
towards improved performance. This method is called Stochastic Gradient Descent which relies on a
method called Back Propagation. This section takes us shortly through the mathematical background
of Stochastic Gradient Decent.
Stochastic gradient decent (SGD) [29] is an iterative method of finding the minimum of a loss
function. If we imagine a loss function as visualized in Figure 4.5, the goal (ideally) of the function is to
optimize the weights to find the global minimum of the loss function. If hypothetically, we are placed
at the brown dot in the figure, we change the weights to take a step in the negative direction of the
gradient (red arrow) of the function at precisely that point. Mathematically this can be described as:
wnew = w −η∇ℓ(w)
(4.30)
Where w is the weights, η is the step size, ∇is the gradient operator of the function ℓ(w).
SGD is
the method used for updating the weights. Back propagation is used to find the gradient of the loss
function. As shown in the simple network in Figure 4.3 and equation (4.25), every node other than the
input node is dependent on something previous, and the output node is dependent on a series of inputs.
The method used to update the weight is called back propagation, and it works by the the chain rule
for differentiation [29].
==PAGE:33==
4.2 Neural Networks
24
Figure 4.5: The figure illustration the loss for different values of weights. The green is the area of the
loss as a function of weight. The red arrow is the gradient of the brown point, and A is the minimum
of the loss function
4.2.5
Convolutional neural network
Convolutional Neural Networks are similar to feed forward neural networks, the difference being that
it must contain at least one convolutional layer. A CNN usually consists of three operations: i) Con-
volution, ii) activation function, and iii) pooling. Convolution is, as the name suggests, when a filter
of size n × n is convoluted with the input (often an image), which transforms the input features into a
feature matrix. An illustration of this is seen in Figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: An illustration of a convolutional layer. An 6x6 image is convoluted with a 3x3 filter,
resulting in a 4x4 feature output. Image from [31]
Step ii) is applying an activation function to the feature matrix. Finally, iii), Pooling is often used
to reduce the dimensions of the input while still keeping information. Commonly two methods are
used; Max pooling and Average pooling. Pooling refers to a pool of values, and ’max’ refers to taking
the maximum value in that pool - where average pooling takes the average. An illustration of average
pooling can be seen in Figure 4.7
When performing convolution, a number of perimeters have to be chosen; the dimensions of the
filters, the number of filters, the stride (for moving the filter horizontally and vertically on the image),
and padding. The illustration in Figure 4.6 uses stride of (1,1), filter size of (3,3) and no padding.
==PAGE:34==
4.3 Interpretable AI
25
Figure 4.7: An example of average pooling. Image from [31]
Whereas the pooling function uses stride of (2,2), filter size of (2,2) - which reduces the original image
of size 6 × 6 to 3 × 3.
4.2.6
Evaluation methods
The evaluation method for the models is the concordance index, which was thoroughly explained in
section 4.1.1.1.
4.3
Interpretable AI
A large portion of this project is regarding interpretability of AI. A number of methods have been
developed to determine how each feature effect the output of the neural network. Methods such as:
LIME, SHAP, permutation feature importance, leave one out, and more. In this project the SHAP
methodology was selected for ranking feature importance. It works by computing Shapely Values, which
is a game-theoretical approach to determine feature importance. This section gives a short introduction
to the theory and methods of the SHAP framework.
4.3.1
Shapley
Shapley is based on cooperative game theory which investigates how groups interact and cooperate to
reach an optimal outcome for everyone [32]. SHAP, in itself, is a library for Python, which can be
implemented and applied to neural networks to give an insight into the importance of the network’s
input features by assigning an importance score to each feature, known as their Shapley value.
To give a quick example on how the Shapley Values are computed, we look at the scenario of three
people sharing a cab home from a night out. We assume that person A’s house is on the way home to
B, and B’s house is on the way home to C, therefore the taxi will pass both A’s and B’s house on the
way home to C’s house. Also, we assume the cab fare to person A’s house is $100, to B’s house is $200
and to C’s house is $600 (see Figure 4.8 for illustration).
The Shapely value is calculated as the average of the marginal contribution of each person across all
permutations of person. Let’s say that person B initially decides to take a cab home alone (cost $200),
and afterwards person A and person C decide that they want to join as well. The price for person A for
joining the cab is $0 as it is already on the way, whereas the expense for person C joining the cab is $400.
The ordering in which they decide to join the ride home therefore matters. The marginal contribution
for that setup is, therefore: $0 for A, $200 for B and $400 for C. In Table 4.2 the calculations for all
permutations of the three people are listed.
==PAGE:35==
4.3 Interpretable AI
26
Figure 4.8: A simple cooperative game theory example, displaying three people (A,B,C), taking a
taxi home, with the cost displaying the price for the fare to the house.
Table 4.2: The marginal contribution of every permutation in the Taxi setup, inspired by [33]
π
A
B
C
(A,B,C)
100
100
400
(A,C,B)
100
0
500
(B,A,C)
0
200
400
(B,C,A)
0
200
400
(C,A,B)
0
0
600
(C,B,A)
0
0
600
ϕ(mean)
33.34
83.34
483.34
The average marginal contribution for person A, B, and C, should therefore be $33.34, $83.34, and
$483.34, respectively. This is the fairest way to split the taxi fare. This method is a simplification
where each permutation includes all three players without the possibility of only two people sharing the
cab or any of the people taking the cab home alone.
In relation to the a deep learning model, the input parameters are the ’persons’. The input param-
eters then collaborate to achieve a minimum loss [32].
When computing the Shapley value, it is important to include the permutations of the subgroups
as well. This is done using the value function Λ:
The Shapley value for a feature is the contribution of the payout summed over all permutations.
ϕj(Λ) =
X
S⊆{1,...,N}\{j}
|S|!(N −|S| −1)!
N!
(Λ(S ∪{j}) −Λ(S)),
(4.31)
where N is the number of features, S is a subgroup of those features, j is the subset of features not in S,
and (Λ(S ∪{j}) −Λ(S)) describes the marginal contribution when j is added to S. This is weighted by
the |S|! ways the set could have been formed. Furthermore weighting by the ways the remaining players
could have been added. And of course summing over all combinations of coalitions (sumS⊆{1,...,p}\{j}),
finally dividing by the total number of orderings ( 1
N!).
4.3.2
SHAP
The Shapely value assigns contribution to the entire feature vector. Whereas SHAP has the possibility
to perform local interpretation. Meaning that from a deep learning model, f(x), the individual feature
importances can be computed. I.e. if we were a bank, and wished to make a model that estimated
the risk of giving a person a bank loan. A typical feature importance model gives a general feature
importance.
However, for a single person, the reason why that person gets a high risk might be
different than some other persons. The SHAP gives the insights to look into importance, as well as
global importance. They proposed a method of modifying equation (4.31) to be:
ϕj(f, x) =
X
z′⊆x′
|z′|! (N −|z′| −1)!
N!
[fx (z′) −fx (z′\j)]
(4.32)
==PAGE:36==
4.3 Interpretable AI
27
Here x′ is know as the ’simplified input’, where x = hx(x′). hx converts binary values to original
inputs. If x’(n) = 1, then it indicates that the input takes it’s original form, and 0 indicates that it takes
a reference value. z’ is a subset of x’, and fx(z′) = f(hx(z′)). ϕj refers only to the feature importance
for single feature, j.
One of the beauties of using the SHAP framework is that it has additivity properties. Meaning that:
f(x) = ϕ0(f, 0) +
M
X
i=1
ϕi(f, x)
(4.33)
In the bank scenario, f(x) is the ’risk’ to give the loan to a person with features x. ϕ0 is the baseline
risk (average risk of all patients), and M is the number of features.(4.31)
This is the methodology that is used to interpret the feature importance.
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 5
Methods
This chapter introduces the methods used in the project. First, the data used and the general charac-
teristics are explained. Later an explanation of how the frequency content of the data was extracted.
Hereafter, an explanation of how the temporal features were extracted and the mathematics behind
most features. Lastly, the process for evaluating the frequency features and the performance of the
selected deep learning models.
The feature extraction has been performed using Matlab and python for feature evaluation, finally,
R has been used to evaluate the models. All relevant code can be seen on GitHub [34].
5.1
Data
A total of 8700 people were evaluated in this project.
Patients were included in the project, split
between two cohorts.
From 1995 to 1998, data from 5793 nocturnal PSGs from both male and female patients were recorded
in the Sleep Heart Health Study (SHHS)[35], and from 2003 to 2005, 2907 nocturnal PSGs from male
patients were recorded in The Study of Osteoporotic Fractures in Men (MrOs)[36]. The requirement
for the selected cohorts was a readily available dataset, with questionnaires that contained PSG’s,
questionnaires that answered disease-related questions, and follow-up time. The combination of the
two cohorts resulted in recordings with a total sleep time of 37796.27 Hours, split between the sleep
stages: REM: 6330.24 Hours, N3: 5099.28 Hours, N2: 17966.34 Hours, N1: 1710.51 Hours, and Wake:
6689.90 Hours.
5.1.1
Generalization of data
For the data to be comparable between the cohorts, they were all set to the same standard.
The
sampling rate between the modality channels were different between cohorts; see Section 5.1.1.
The MrOs cohort was resampled to fit the sampling frequency of the SHHS. Before doing so, the
signals were low-pass filtered just before half the sampling frequency of each modality of SHHS. The
filtering type was an IIR, and Matlabs [37] filtfilt function was used to remove group delay. The filtering
ensures minimal aliasing, which would distort the signal.
Table 5.1: The sampling frequency for both cohorts
Modality
Sampling Frequency

==PAGE:38==
5.2 Frequency extraction
29
Imputation
From the questionnaires provided by the cohorts there were 1.3% of the data missing ( 868 missing values
65292 total values ).
As the percentage of data that was missing was so low, I decided that imputing the dataset would
not cause too high a degree of bias. Univariate feature imputation only looks at the same feature to
estimate the missing value, i.e., replacing the missing value with a mean. However, I decided to use
sklearn’s Multivariate Imputer (IterativeImputer) [38]. It is a machine learning model where the target
parameter is a column with missing values, starting with the one with the least missing values. It
then uses the other columns as predictor variables. As the model was not perfect, it sometimes yielded
non-categorical results for categories variables. All categorical features were therefore rounded so it
would fit the rest of the data.
A summary of the questionnaire data after imputation can be seen in Table 5.2
Table 5.2: A table stating mean, standard standard deviation, minimum and maximum for the covari-

5.2
Frequency extraction
In standard practice, the EEG frequency domain is used to determine sleep stages. In this project, the
research is focused on the frequency content within each sleep stage, for the EEG, EOGs, and EMG
modalites. This section describes how the frequency content has been extracted.
5.2.1
Welch’s Periodogram
The Welch method [39] was selected instead of simply using the Fourier transform, as it is better at
generalizing the frequency content and makes the signal smooth. It splits the signal in segments, which
are windowed, and then Fourier transformed.
Let the signal be: x(n), n = 0, ..., N −1. By splitting the signal into m blocks of length L.
x1 = w(n) · x(n)
x2 = w(n) · x(n + D)
...
...
...
xk = w(n) · x(n + m · D)
(5.1)
==PAGE:39==
5.3 Time domain feature extraction
30
Where,
n = 0, · · · , L −1
m = 0, · · · , L
(5.2)
Where L is the length of the segment, and D is the distance between two starting points (overlap).
For each xk, a window-vector, w(n), is applied, which usually is selected to have a ’gaussian’ shape,
with the max amplitude of 1. This minimizes the edges of the signal, thereby reducing spectral leakage
when performing the Fourier transform. The discrete Fourier Transform is calculated for each xk:
Ak(n) = 1
L ·
L−1
X
j=0
xk(j) · w(j) · exp−2 π i j n/L
(5.3)
where i = sqrt(−1). A K number of periodograms are defined as:
Ik(fn) = L
U |Ak(n)|2
k = 1, 2, · · · , K
(5.4)
Where fn = n/L, n = 0, ..., L/2 describes the frequency at bin n.
U is defined as the squared sum of the window function divided by the length:
U = 1
L
L−1
X
j=0
W 2(j)
(5.5)
Averaging over the periodograms concludes Welch’s method.
ˆ
PSD (fn) = 1
K
K
X
k=1
Ik (fn)
(5.6)
A number of decisions have to be made when deciding to use Welch’s method. Overlap, window
length, and type of window. All decisions change the output of the Power Spectral Density (PSD)
function.
In this project, the Hamming Window was selected [40] with an overlap of 75% [41, 42, 43]. In
order to check for the highest performance, a number of segment lengths were selected.
For EEG,
EMG, Electroocoulogram Right (EOGR), and Electroocoulogram Left (EOGL), the window lengths
from 1s to 10s were selected. By increasing the window length, there is increased spectral precision.
That results in increased features, which would increase computation time and interpretability for the
models - therefore, a double-edged sword.
Patients have varying lengths, amounts, and epochs of sleep stages. Welch’s method was performed
on every sleep-stage segmented by two other sleep stages. A duration weighted average of the PSG was
then performed within all sleep stages to ensure the longest segments had the most influence on the
average PSG.
5.3
Time domain feature extraction
For the time domain feature extraction, Matlab was used. In this section, the formulas and reasoning
behind these features are described. All features were analyzed from the start to end of each sleep stage.
Each feature was calculated within each sleep stage, and it was averaged by duration for each segment.
==PAGE:40==
5.3 Time domain feature extraction
31
5.3.1
EEG features
EEG signals can be chaotic for the human eye to interpret. By using feature extraction finding the
entropy, energy, and other features describing the signal quantitatively is possible to explain what is
important to look at within the EEG.
The selection of EEG features was inspired by [44].
5.3.1.1
Entropy and energy
Entropy is a measure of disorder or randomness in a signal [45], whereas energy contains information
related to the signal power and amplitude.
Shannon Entropy is the most common entropy, and it is defined as:

5.3.2
SaO2, Eye Movements, and Time Features
This section explains the feature extraction method used for Eye Movements (EM) detection, SaO2
Feature extraction, and the time spent in each sleep stage.
Figure 5.1: Eye movement detection diagram - Inspired by [60]
Eye Movements (EM) were quantified by using a slightly modified method proposed by Julie
A.E. Christensen [60] in 2017. The methodology is based on 7 steps as seen in Figure 5.1. The method
uses the left and right EOG. As the eyes move in the same direction when moved, the EOG of the two
signals are in anti-phase.
Step [1] consists of pre-processing the EOG data and streamlining between cohorts. MrOs had a
sampling rate of 256 Hz and SHHS 50 Hz. The first step was therefore to streamline the two datasets
by downsampling the EOGs from MrOs to 50 Hz. Secondly, a high-pass filter with a cutoff at 0.1 Hz
was performed to remove the baseline drift of both EOGs.
In order to extract both frequency and time information, the EOG channels were decomposed by
using the Dual-Tree Complex Wavelet Transforms (DTCWT) with Farra’s filters and Kingsbury Q-shift
with 14 taps. This results in two complex wavelets coeﬀicients:
dR
c (j, n) = dR
r (j, n) + idR
i (j, n)
(5.24)
dL
c (j, n) = dL
r (j, n) + idL
i (j, n)
(5.25)
Where dr and di denote the real and imaginary coeﬀicients respectively. The coeﬀicients [6,7,8,9,10]
are the only ones kept, as they are coeﬀicients representing frequencies between 0.125Hz and 4 Hz (Eye
movement frequency).
Equations 5.24 and 5.25 are both complex, and can therefore be translated into angles. The difference
between the two EOGs was thresholded at 0.90πRad with all values below being set to zero. The
DTCWT was then reconstructed, and values exceeding 600 µV were considered artifacts and removed.
The two signals are anti-phasic under eye movement, and the difference between the two channels
are calculated:
Adiff =| EOGLClean −EOGRClean |
(5.26)
The 92nd percentile was found and used as a threshold. Every instance where Adiff surpassed the
threshold was declared as a candidate for EM. Two eye movement segments were combined if the time
==PAGE:43==
5.3 Time domain feature extraction
34
between them were less than 2 seconds (EMCand1 ). Lastly, if the total duration of one segment was
less than 2.5 seconds, they were removed, resulting in a vector denoting when eye movement is present
or not.
The eye movements were then split into each sleep stage and normalized by the duration of each
sleep stage.
In figure 5.2 these steps are visualized for a one-minute interval for REM. In the Appendix (section 8),
similar plots are shown for the other sleep stages.
Figure 5.2: Eye Movement detection using the methodology from [60]. Starting with the Raw signal,
which is cleaned, and the two signals are subtracted from each other. Thereafter candidates are found
for eye movement.
The segments are then cleaned up dependent on their duration.
The final eye
movement vector is seen in second to the bottom.
SaO2 were treated by finding the average oxygen concentration, and by counting how often the
SaO2 was below either 90%, 80%, or 70%.
Time in each sleep stage was manually calculated by annotations from the cohorts.
5.3.3
ECG Features
All ECG features are based on the RR interval, which were extracted by using the Pan-Thomkins algo-
rithm. The algorithm used was invented by Jiapu Pan and Willis J. Tompkins in 1985, and the Matlab
code used was written by Hooman Sedghamiz [61]. From the RR intervals, the features were extracted
using Marcus Vollmer’s Matlab code [62].
The Heart Rate (Hr) is a measure of how many times the heart pumps per minute. From the RR
interval, the average Hr is calculated as:

(5.27)
Where RR is the R-R-intervals in minutes, N is the number of R-R intervals, and 60 indicates the scalar
from R-R interval to heart rate.
SDNN is the standard deviation between the RR intervals. This is a good measure for the variance
in the heartbeats.

Triangular Index (TRI) and Triangular Interpolation (TINN) are both derived from the

More statistical features are included for ECG features; Kurtosis, Skewness, Variance, RMS of the
RR intervals, and the Std of SSD (SDSD).

5.3.5
Project process for frequency features
To achieve the goal of understanding which frequencies are important for survival, deep learning, and
Cox were utilized. In Figure 5.4 the overall process for the frequency analysis in this thesis is outlined.
Usually when having a single feature, the Cox model would be used directly. The power spectrum
contains lots of information, but there is likely a specific power band with importance and not a single
frequency. The deep learning results allow us to split the frequency bins according to how deep learning
uses the input features. This finally allows for to perform Cox analysis on the results.
Figure 5.4: This figure illustrates how we got from the data to the project’s final results. Starting
with deep learning and then using the knowledge from deep learning in the Cox Model.
The first step was the data pre-processing covered in Section 5.2. This gives us the frequency content
for each sleep stage for the modalities EEG, EMG, EOGL, and EOGR.
These modalities and sleep stages were used to make a deep learning model that could estimate com-
plex structures. When constructing a deep learning network, many things must be considered when
optimizing the model. One of the things that were taken into consideration was which model type to
use. Here we tested two models, the FFNN and the CNN. The FFNN was the primary model, partly
used for other optimization parameters. The input to a CNN is usually an image, and FFNN a vector.
Therefore, the data was split as seen in Figure 5.5, with CNN having the dimension (5,N), and FFNN
(1,5 · N) where N was the length of the power spectrum for each modality.
5.3.5.1
FFNN
Several considerations are to be made when building a well-performing FFNN; what should the learning
rate, L1-coeﬀicient, and batch size be? A simple model was set up to find this, and a grid search began.
==PAGE:47==
5.3 Time domain feature extraction
38
Figure 5.5: An illustration of how the input changes according to whether it is CNN or FFNN that
is used.
This was tested on a simple structure, with an input layer (Nodes: 1245), three hidden layers (Nodes:
128,64,34), and an output layer (1 Node). The model was evaluated based on the EEG modality with
the Welch’s periodogram options of; 5-second windows, with 75% overlap, and a Hamming window.
The activation function used was ReLU, with a dropout of 20%. The grid-search values were:
• L1= [0.1, 0.01, 0.005, 0.001, 0.0005, 0.0001]
• Learning Rate = [1, 0.1, 0.01, 0.005, 0.001, 0.0005, 0.0001]
• Batch Size= [32, 64, 128, 256, 512, 1024]
To reduce computation time, an early stopping mechanism was set. If there was no improvement
over the last ten epochs the model training was terminated.
A total of 252 (6 · 7 · 6) models were trained showing that a learning rate, L1 coeﬀicient, and batch
size of; 0.001, 0.001, and 128, respectively, had the best performance. These parameters were used for
further analysis.
Finding best window length in Welch’s Method
To increase performance, Welch’s window length was modified. This was tested by running 10 itera-
tions with 10 window lengths ranging from 1 to 10 seconds for the MrOs cohort. The results can be
seen in Figure 5.6, where the two green XX’s show which model had the highest Concordance Index (CI).
This result showed that either the best performance was at the end of the spectrum or in the middle.
The same analysis was performed on the SHHS data, and the results can be seen in Figure 5.7
The two cohorts agreed on the window length from EEG and EOGL. For EOGR and EMG, there
was a disagreement. To proceed, it was decided to choose the lowest Window Length with the highest
performance. This was chosen because the lower window length would result in fewer features, decrease
computation time and reduce over-fitting. This resulted in a final decision on the best window lengths
- see Table 5.4
==PAGE:48==
5.3 Time domain feature extraction
39
Figure 5.6: Finding the best model performance in relation to Window Length for MrOs. The green
xx show where the highest performance was for each modality
Figure 5.7: Finding the best model performance in relation to Window Length for SHHS. The green
xx show where the highest performance was for each modality
==PAGE:49==

Figure 5.8: Boxplot visualizing the CI performance between the MrOs, SHHS, and combined, using
the optimal window length for each of the cohorts.
The comparison between the models is shown based on the CI in Figure 5.8. The model becomes
increasingly better with more data. However, in combined EEG, the performance is only the second
highest. The Concordance Index (CI) estimate or partial likelihood are not a suitable parameters to
use as a comparison between different datasets, as it is highly reliant on the distribution of censored
and non-censored patients. As a note, the dropout was increased to 50% as the combined model tended
to overfit, and the batch size was increased to 256 to reduce computation time.
The model can, however, be compared across modalities. These show that the EMG has the lowest
performance across, followed by the EEG and the EOG’s with the highest performance.
5.3.6
CNN
A similar process was performed for the CNN, excluding the window length selection. However, a set of
network structures were tested and evaluated. The best performing CNN structure has been visualized
for the EEG channel in Figure 5.9.
The performance for each modality using this structure has been visualized in Figure 5.10. It shows
that EOGL has the highest performance of 71.4%, therafter EEG of 70.7%, EOGR 70.6%, and EMG
with a low of 62.8%. As the CNN structure yielded higher accuracy than the FFNN, it was chosen for
further analysis in this project.
