CHAPTER 6
Results
In this Chapter, the results of the project are presented. The results follow the project process explained
in Chapter 4. A deep learning model was constructed in the previous section, whereas in this section the
SHapley Additive exPlanations is utilized to understand the impact of the feature inputs. Combining a
set of SHAP gives an indication of the influence of specific frequency bands which are then empirically
determined. These bands are then used to be computed by the Cox model. Finally, the results of the
time domain features using Cox are explained.
6.1
Survival analysis using deep learning
The SHAP framework allows us to interpret our deep learning results. The SHAP values were extracted
using the Sherlock High-Performance Computing (HPC) (Taking 20 hours for EMG, 24 for EOG’s, and
28 for EEG).
In Figure 6.1 and Figure 6.2 an example of two power spectrums for EEG are shown. One for a
patient who has been recorded to die early, and another who participated in the study the longest and
was still alive at the time of gathering data.
Figure 6.1:
An example of a power spectrum for a patient who dies soon after the recording is
made.Pink indicates that that frequency increases survival, whereas teal indicates the opposite
==PAGE:52==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
43
Figure 6.2: An example of a power spectrum for a patient who has been in the experiment for the
longest time and has not died.
Pink indicates that that frequency increases survival, whereas teal
indicates the opposite
The figures show the power spectrum with an overlay of the SHAP value. When it is bright pink,
it indicates that specific features attributed positively to the person’s survival, whereas bright blue
indicates a negative impact. Looking at Figure 6.1 (Early Death), it is seen how Wake and N1 are
primarily bright blue, and REM is a bit darker. With N3 and N2 being mostly purple, indicating that
they contribute barely anything to the indication of living long or short.
Opposite to this is the person who lives for a long time. All the sleep stage spectrums are on the
brighter side of purple, which corresponds to him living longer.
These results are diﬀicult to interpret. Even though the results are clear, it is diﬀicult to generalize
what ’exactly’ causes some parts to be important and others not. The results are, therefore, generalized
as presented in Figure 6.3. This is a SHAP Beeswarm plot of the EEG (The other modalities can be
seen in the Appendix section 8)
==PAGE:53==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
44
Figure 6.3: SHAP values based on a deep learning model of EEG. If the SHAP value is positive, it
indicates longer survival, whereas a negative SHAP value indicates shorter survival time. The color
pink indicates a high power spectrum value, and teal a low value
The Beeswarm plot was achieved by calculating SHAP values for all frequencies for the entire test
set. The y axis corresponds to the SHAP value, and bright pink and blue indicate a high and low power
Spectrum value, respectively. The power spectrum values have been normalized across all patients to
understand the output fully.
The Beeswarm plot has some pros and cons. The result gives a good indication of what the model
is looking into. However, one main problem persists. How do we explain and generalize what is going
on to medical professionals?
The solution is to split the frequencies into segments based on the SHAP results and utilize the
Cox Proportional Hazards framework. Currently, there is a chance the model is trying to model if
the person is old or male, which is well known as having increased mortality risk. By using the Cox
framework, this can be taken into account. By doing so, we have an interpretable result and can take
covariates into account.
In this Cox model the bands are evaluated by themselves (named Single Effect analysis). The hazard
function which the Cox regression is trying to fit is, i.e.:
h(t) = h0 · ef(β,X) = h0 · e(β1·xEEG[REM(15 Hz - 33 Hz)]+B·Xc)
(6.1)
where B is a set of β’s corresponding to the set of Confounders (Xc)., and xEEG[REM(15 Hz - 33 Hz)] is
the median of the frequencies from 15 Hz to 33 Hz in the REM sleep stage of EEG.
An illustration of how the segments are split is seen in Figure 6.4. The width of each bar indicates
the area which the median has been taken. The height indicates the confidence interval (95%), and the
color is green if it is significant, and red if not. This has been plotted above the SHAP model, giving a
visual perspective on how the bins have been made. Every segmentation was split manually using the
Beeswarm Plot.
==PAGE:54==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
45
Figure 6.4: A combination of the SHAP Beeswarm plot and the Cox model. The width of each band
shows which section the median has been found, the color green shows significance, whereas does not.
This is just a visualization of the binning process.
Four models were tested, each with increasingly aggregated confounders typically used within sleep
research. The confounders used for the four models are listed in Table 4.1.
The results for EEG, EMG, and EOG’s are presented in the following subsections.
6.1.1
EEG
The results for every frequency bin for the EEG model are visualized in Figure 6.5. A quick reminder
before we start to interpret the results; As stated in the background section of PSG’s (Section 2.1.1),
the frequency content of the brain changes with both gender and age. What we are trying to model is
not survivability based on gender or age. Therefore, we are investigating what is significant between
all models, as this tells something about the survivability independently of the confounders, which are
known to be directly related to mortality. Cox models can be challenging to interpret in themselves,
especially when there are many features to evaluate - Therefore I suggest that the reader goes through
these sections slowly, as it otherwise can be diﬀicult to understand.
==PAGE:55==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning

Figure 6.5: This figure shows the output of the Cox models across all selected frequency bins for
EEG. Mx indicates the model type, the color of each dot indicates the HR, whereas the size indicates
the p-value. The empty square indicates that the corresponding frequency bin did not yield significant
results.
In Appendix (Chapter 8), a detailed version of the same figure is illustrated with the Hazard Ra-
tio (HR) and Confidence interval - the ’circle-plot’ is, however, better for a quick overview of what is
happening across all models.
For model 1, the results seem to be somewhat consistent across all five sleep stages. It is character-
ized mainly by protective scores in the lower end of the frequency spectrum and increased risk in the
higher end. This is not surprising since we generally see a movement of power towards the higher fre-
quencies with age, which directly correlates with mortality. To counter this, the second model includes
age and sex as confounding factors and it becomes apparent from the figure that the response changes.
==PAGE:56==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
47
Now, let us dig into the sleep results one by one, starting by explaining REM (Figure 6.5a). It shows
that the frequencies at 14.75 −33.5 Hz are significant throughout all models. The Hazard Ratio(HR
for that segment is blue, indicating the HR < 1. An increase in the 14.75 −33.5 Hz within REM is,
therefore, protective for mortality. The detailed figure (Appendix Figure 9) shows that the Hazard ratio
for REM 14.75 −33.5 Hz for model 4 is 0.88 (CI95% 0.83-0.92), indicating that for every one decrease
in standard deviation, there is a 12% increase in death at any point. The same effect is seen for similar
frequencies in N1 (13.5-30 Hz), having an HR of 0.91 (CI95% 0.86 - 0.97).
REM Sleep at 1-2 Hz is significant with an HR < 1 in Model 1, however, in models 2,3, and 4, the
HR shifts and becomes >1. Whenever seeing results like this, we should be cautious about reading too
much into it, but the interpretation of this is quite clear. In the general population, when the 1-2 Hz
are compared across patients, the ones with lower values are more likely to die - the reason is the same
as explained above; it describes a general difference between genders and age. However, when taking
that into account, within those age and gender groups, the increased 1-2 Hz band describes a higher
chance of mortality (HR: 1.07 (CI95% 1.02-1.13)).
N1 at 5.75 to 7.75 Hz also has HR > 1 across all models, and the same tendency is seen for Wake for
the frequencies 4.0 - 8.25 Hz. N1 and Wake have an HR of 1.05 (CI95%1.0 - 1.09) and 1.05 (CI95%1.01
- 1.1), respectively.
The analysis shows that N2 and N3 are protective for increasing band power for the frequencies 9.5-15.0
Hz (N2) and 9.75 - 10.75 Hz (N3). N2’s and N3’s are also similar in the frequency range 1.75 to 3.75
Hz, showing an HR < 1, suggesting protective behavior for increased band power. K-Complexes are [7]
usually found between 1.6 and 4.0 Hz. K-complexes are often found in combination with sleep spindles,
which have frequencies between 11 - 16 Hz [66]. Both features are found in the N2 sleep stage. These
frequencies indicate that sleep spindles and k-complexes have positive impact on survival. This was
somewhat expected, as they are both associated with positive attributes such as memory and motor
ability [67].
Summary: The higher frequencies ∼(14.75 - 33.5) for REM and N1 are protective, as well as 8.25-
15 Hz for N1 and N2 indicate prolonged survival for increasing bands power.
The lower frequency band at N3 seems to be important in survival according to N3 (0.75-4 Hz) (HR <
1). Increasing band power for the frequencies ∼4-8.25 Hz (Wake and N1), 0-0.5 Hz (N3), and 1-2 Hz
(REM) are all suggestive of increasing mortality.
6.1.2
EOG R & EOG L
A similar analysis is performed for the EOG channels. The analysis is based on what the left and
right EOG channels have in common. As eyes usually move in the same direction simultaneously, the
frequency content regarding the eyes should be the same. If something is significant on one side and
not the other, it is possibly a confounding effect, i.e., EEG, how we sleep (right, left, stomach, back),
or movement in general. The goal is to find information that is only relevant to eye movement. The
result arising from the Cox analysis can be seen in Figure 6.6 and Figure 6.7 (Appendix section 8 for
detailed table)
In general, the EOG channels do not seem to have many areas that are significant across all models.
Only three bandwidths have the same outcome across the two channels. The Wake stage, for ∼1.5
(EOGR 1.25 - 1.75 Hz, EOGL 1 - 1.75 Hz), and for the N1 stage ∼0.75 Hz (EOGR 0.5 - 0.75 Hz, EOGL
0.75 - 0.75 Hz) in common. Both of these areas have an HR > 1. The ∼1.5 Hz in Wake is not significant
in EEG, but ∼0.75 Hz in N1 sleep is right on the border of being significant, thus concluding that this
effect does arise from the eye movement. The other significant areas are mostly also significant within
EEG, which might explain the result.
==PAGE:57==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
48
Those frequencies at ∼0.75 Hz are within the usual frequency range of eye movement. Wake at
∼1.5 Hz is also a somewhat realistic speed. However, it is eye movement that translates to 1.5 times
a second. Understanding why this is significant might be a bit diﬀicult. Before we over-analyze this
section, let us look more directly into the eye-movement analysis made later in 6.2.4.

6.1.4
Using patient-specific baseline
The results above do suggest that it is primarily the EEG channels which has a direct effect on mortality.
In this sub-section, the thought process was to use every patient as a baseline for themselves. As N2
had just about three times more recording time than the other sleep stages, that was primarily used as
==PAGE:60==
6.1 Survival analysis using deep learning
51
a reference.
The way it was calculated was by subtracting REM from e.g. N2 sleep. (denoted REM-N2). This
was used as an input for a deep learning structure like the models above. The Concordance Index for
the models can be seen in Figure 6.9. The results show that N2, in itself, is the sleep stage which
accounts for the highest accuracy from the EEG. When subtracting N2 from REM, the CI is 67.8%,
and for Wake - N2, the performance increases to 68.6. The difference between REM and Wake had the
lowest performance of 65.7. These initial results show no increased value when doing the subtraction
instead of ’just’ using a baseline. A simple test is also included in the figure, where only REM and N2
are included in a CNN, excluding the other stages - which obtained an accuracy of 69.9%.
Figure 6.9: This figure shows a set of Concordance indices—first, the N2 and REM by themselves.
Then N2 is subtracted from REM (REM - N2), for Wake - N2, and Wake - REM. Lastly, the performance
using REM as well as N2 (REM + N2).
The previous analysis showed that it was the REM and N2 that was of the highest significance after
taking confounders into consideration. Therefore a Cox analysis is performed in the same manner as in
the section above.
The result is seen in Figure 6.10. It show that an increased difference between 15-33.5 Hz is protec-
tive, whereas an increased difference between 1-1.5 Hz and 3.5-4.25 Hz increases mortality. One could
speculate whether this is a result of the difference between the two or if it arises from those bands being
significant in REM and N2.
6.1.5
Time-Dependent Concordance index
The concordance index is a good way of evaluating the model’s entirety. However, it does not accurately
give an understanding of which patient groups it is accurate for and which it is not. Here we investigate
the time-dependent concordance index that I have developed. This is a concordance index which has the
patients split into equally sized groups (see Figure 6.11). In the example seen in the figure, a prediction
was made using the test set using the FFNN EEG structure. The figure shows the distribution of
censored vs. non-censored patients (died) and the concordance index for a select group. Every group is
the same size (169 ± 1 patients). The analysis shows that the concordance index is low for patients who
have participated in the study for 0-2864 days and 5356-6158 days. However, it seems to be reliable-
between 2865-5355 days. Looking at the number of censored patients in the 0-2864 days group, it is
understandable that the model does not have high accuracy here, as there is close to 90% censoring.
The age distribution explains the low concordance index for 5356-6158 days, suggesting that the model

If the focus of the study was to achieve the best performance of a deep learning model, this element
could be used to evaluate and optimize the model. The age distribution could be swapped with another
confounder, i.e., gender, apnea, or alcohol, which could give another interpretation or ensure the user
that the model is not overfitting to some specific confounder. This figure, therefore, explains quite well
’why’ the performance is the way it is.

6.2 Time Domain Cox Analysis
53
6.2
Time Domain Cox Analysis
The frequency content does tell a lot about the content of a signal; however, features extracted from
the time domain carry temporal aspects, which might be valuable in the description of survival.
How and why the selected features were chosen has been explained in Section 5.3, and this section is
the result of a Cox Analysis based upon every single feature. The evaluation was performed in the same
manner as the Deep Learning Result section above, using this Single Effect analysis method, where we
have one feature we want to investigate and a set of confounders that adjust for such as age, sex, and
diseases. Four models were again selected, stated in Table 4.1.
6.2.1
EEG
All of the EEG Cox results can be found in Section 6.2.1 as a dot plot, with the corresponding detailed
plot in Chapter 8. For an easy comparison, I have selected seven features that were of interest across
all models and modalities - see Figure 6.12. A short recap of these variables. The median is an estimate
of the intensity of the EEG signal. This is seen as being important in REM and N1. The Log Energy
Entropy is the sum of the signal squared - resulting in a good measure for the signal strength. It shows
that increased Log Entropy for REM, N1, and Wake have increased mortality risk.
The Log Root Sum of Sequential Variation measures the square difference between two points
throughout each segment. It is significant in REM, N1, and Wake. It does, however, become significant
for REM after adjusting for the amount of REM sleep. The Log variation has not been normalized
for segment length, meaning that it results in a higher number for longer segments. We know that in-
creased Wake time increases mortality (also explained later) - which explains why Wake and N1 become
significant for Log variation.
The Second Diff and First Diff explain the entire waveform length - those are not significant; but,
when standardizing by dividing by the standard deviation, it becomes significant in REM, N2, and N1.
The Hjorth Complexity represents the changes in frequency. If there are many changes, the complexity
is high. Essentially it means how close to a sinusoid the signal is. Thereby concluding that if the signal
contains a high degree of variation, it increases mortality in REM, N3, N2, and N1.
Hjorth Mobility represents the average frequency of the signal - REM, N2, and N1 all indicate that
higher frequency content is better for survival, whereas the Wake stage indicates that lower frequencies
are better. The results here do correspond to what was observed in the frequency analysis. The second
and first differences, mobility and complexity, all explain the frequency content. The Second Diff N
and First Diff N and Hjorth mobility explain how a higher frequency signal is more protective for REM,
N2, and N1, which is precisely what was seen in the frequency analysis. The same tendency with the
Wake stage for Hjorth mobility, which explains a lower frequency is protective for survival.
The Entropy features were not selected as there were many which primarily had the same outcome.
Entropy is a way of explaining the distribution of the EEG signal. Disorder in a signal corresponds to
a high Entropy. Both Wake and REM show that if there is a high disorder in the signal, it provides
increased mortality risk.

6.2 Time Domain Cox Analysis

6.2.2
ECG
Moving on to the ECG features as seen in Figure 6.14 (Chapter 8 for a detailed format). It seems there
is no difference between the sleep stages. (Note, HR in the figures is heart hate, not Hazard Ratio.
When referring to the feature heart rate, the notation Hr is used).
In all of the sleep stages, higher skewness of Hr is protective. This suggests that the mean is to the
right of the mode. This is backed up by a similar result from the average heart rate - the average heart
rate is more descriptive with a Hazard Ratio of ∼1.14, and the skewness with a Hazard Ratio of ∼0.91.
The Root mean square (RMS) for the heart rate is significant, suggesting that a more consistent heart
rate is protective with a hazard ratio of ∼1.14. Interestingly, before adjusting for age and sex, the RMS
and Mean are significant - but with a Hazard ratio < 1. This suggests that it is protective to have a
higher average heart rate - This makes sense, as women generally have higher heart rates than men and
older people generally have lower RMS [68].
The features denoted SDNN, SDSD, RMSSD, pNN50 TRI, and TINN are all features derived from
the R-R intervals, which were derived using the Pan-Tompkins Algorithm. The R-R interval features
generally show the same tendency as the Photoplethysmography (PPG) features, although with lower
power. This suggests that the Pan-Tompkins algorithm was sub-optimal, resulting in a noisy or incorrect
R-R. One thing that appears is that TINN is protective for higher values (for REM, N2, and N1).
Suggesting a broader base in the triangular index is better. This is counter-intuitive as the RMS and
RMSSD show the opposite. Currently, this does not make sense and needs to be investigated more
thoroughly as to why this feature is still significant.

6.2.3
EMG
The results for EMG can be seen in Figure 6.15 (Chapter 8 for detailed information). EMG only has
a single significant feature across all models - the Normalized Waveform Length in the Wake stage—
suggesting that higher frequency content and amplitude of the EMG corresponds to increased mortality.
This is not found in the frequency analysis above, which raises a few questions. It was expected to find
REM sleep without atonia (RSWA) features within the EMG features. RSWA is a feature requirement
for REM sleep Behavior Disorder (RBD). It is defined as an excessive sustained or intermittent elevation
of EMG tone. However, this was not seen in these results - which might be due to the sample population
not including many RBD patients.

6.2.4
More
This section analysis SaO2, Eye movement, and time spent in each sleep stage features. The results
can be seen in Figure 6.16 (Detailed plots can be found in Chapter 8)
The results show that all sleep stages (REM, N3, N2, N1) depend on the number of times the oxy-
gen saturation goes below 90, also when adjusting for the AHI score. Corresponding to this, we have
that increasing SaO2 does increase survival time. However, these two elements are not visible when
adjusting for diseases in the Wake stage. This was an expected outcome, as the body and brain require
high oxygen concentration to function. Surprisingly, SaO2 U70 does not appear significant, but that is
likely a sample size problem.
The ’Time in’ feature relates to the sleep stage within. REM sleep, N2, and Wake are all significant
in Model 3. Model 4 does not make sense as it includes the confounder ’Time in REM’. It is still signifi-
==PAGE:68==
6.2 Time Domain Cox Analysis
59
cant in REM in Model 4 because if two variables are the same, one of them is removed. This corresponds
to the findings of Eileen Leary. Eileen did, however, not include time in Wake when doing this analysis.
The hazard ratio for Wake is the second highest of all the sleep stages in Model 3, suggesting that it is
the second most important feature after REM.
Finally, we have eye movements. The number of eye movements is significant in both REM and
Wake. However, when we normalize for the total duration of each sleep stage, only the N3 becomes
significant. A similar effect was seen in the EOG frequency analysis. It showed that frequencies at
∼0.25 Hz had the highest hazard ratio of 1.19 (1.11 - 1.28), which correlates well with the findings
using the novel eye-movement detector.

==PAGE:69==
6.2 Time Domain Cox Analysis
60
6.2.5
Testing models
To ensure that all confounders fulfill the assumptions of the Cox model, the R framework with the
survival package was used. All confounders used did follow the Cox assumptions. Almost all features
followed the assumptions, and features that did not were further visually investigated to determine if
it made sense to evaluate further that specific feature - in none of the cases it made sense.
Every feature investigated was tested using a log-likelihood ratio test. This was done by having a
model made solely of the confounders, compared to a model consisting of confounders + the specific
feature. The log-likelihood ratio test indicates whether adding a feature changes the model accuracy.
Every feature where the addition added a significant value according to the log-likelihood ratio was
denoted with an *(Asterisk) in the detailed tables in the appendix. This does not change the result in
any way, but it does formally state that the features that have been investigated have similar influence
according to the Cox Hazard and log-likelihood ratio test.
6.2.6
Summary of parameters
We now have 2667 Hazard Ratios, and we understand which parameters influence survival and which
do not. This sub-section tries to evaluate the most important feature. Before doing so, it is good to
have a baseline of a good concordance index. For example, the Hazard Ratio of Age is 1.12 (1.1 - 1.13),
resulting in a concordance index of 76.02%, meaning that a one age increase increases the hazard by
12%. Sex, on the other hand, gives a CI of 58.0% and results in an HR of 0.41 (0.36-0.47). Sex is a
binary variable where 0 is male and 1 is female. This suggests that being a female generally lowers the
risk of death by 59%.
Compared to age and sex, the top 5 highest concordance indices without any adjustments can be seen
in table Table 6.1. Interestingly the best performance, according to my analysis, are the power-band
features. In Model 1, the very low frequencies (0-1.5 Hz) are in focus for the modalities EEG (N2 and
N3), EOG L, and EMG. EEG N3 at (0-0.5) results in a concordance index of 66.0 and a hazard ratio
of 1.36 (CI95% 1.31-1.40). The Concordance index for all features can be found in the supplementary
material (Chapter 8). Beware, this data has not been visualized and is, therefore, a table consisting
of 2667 Concordance indices, Hazard ratios, and confidence intervals. It has been sorted, and if an
investigation of the feature importance is wished, then the table is useful

==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 7
Discussion
I have extracted and investigated 657 frequency and temporal features from the PSG. They were indi-
vidually assessed using the Cox model, which evaluated their importance regarding survival.
When interpreting this amount of features, it can quickly become rather diﬀicult to digest and explain
everything relevant; however, this project managed to handle this exceptionally well. A total of 132
features were found to be significant, while 525 features were deemed insignificant when looking at
their ability to affect survival. Of the 132 features, many were significant across sleep stages. It would
therefore be relevant to explore the features of interest further, in order to figure out exactly why they
are significant to survival.
A hierarchy of survival features was produced, and it showed that the lower frequencies in EEG of
the N3 sleep were most significant, and the REM stage ranged from 14.75 - 33.5 Hz.
The results of this project have raised numerous thoughts, including the ones listed below:
i) An evaluation of the model performance of a deep survival model, when compared to the Cox
model. Currently, the performance of the Cox model proved superior. However, the state-of-the-art
section showed that performance could increase using deep learning. A couple of days at the end of the
project were spent producing a deep learning model using all 657 features - the model highly overfitted
to the training data and resulted in a performance significantly lower than the one achieved by using a
single power spectrum.
ii) The results from the frequency analysis did not completely correspond to the findings in the
SHAP Beeswarm plots. This suggests that relevant interactions between the frequency bands exist.
Considering these interactions, it makes sense that if one power band is high, another must be low, and
the interaction between these two are more accurate predictors than they are individually. I propose
a multi-effect model, where a single Cox model handles multiple frequency bands and covariates. This
allows for interactions across bands. Instead of concluding that the upper beta band in EEG of REM
sleep is significant, a more descriptive conclusion could have been; The ratio between the upper beta and
delta band is the best predictor for mortality.
iii) I have become increasingly comfortable implementing deep learning models throughout this
project. One aspect which could have increased the eﬀiciency, would have been to make use of an-
other method to find the hyperparameters. This would allow for individual hyperparameters for each
modality, which could have slightly increased performance. The current hyperparameter selection was
a grid-search, but using a hyperparameter optimization framework, like Optuna [69], could have saved
me valuable time.
iv) The deep learning models used a loss function based on Cox Proportional Hazards model. The
reason to use Cox is often sparked by a limited amount of data available to explain the trend being
researched. The two studies did include data from 2122 patients who were dead. This should be suf-
ficient data to create, i.e., a four-class model; died between [0-1), [1-2), [2-3) or ≥3 years. This would
likely give a similar result, but it may also result in a higher power and increased interpretability for
the doctors.
==PAGE:72==
7 Discussion
63
v) The Sleep Heart Health Study did not include the reason for death. This information could have
given rise to a greater understanding of the model performance. Some might have died of heart attack,
Alzheimer’s, while others passed as a result of non-medical reasons. The models are probably better
at estimating Heart-attacks and Alzheimers than accidents. With this information, it would therefore
have been possible to gain a highly increased understanding how the model is estimating survival.
vi) A well-known problem within deep learning is that bad input results in sub-optimal performance.
When investigating the power spectrums of the modalities, a number of them were found to include
noise in the spectrums. Some had a power-line noise, while others had unexplainable cyclic noise. The
problem with my analysis is that all frequencies with each sleep stage were normalized to have a sum
of 1. Multiple cyclic artifacts could confound the data, thereby reducing the performance accuracy. A
possible solution could be to remove all patients whose spectrums include noise and try to perform the
same analysis - hopefully reducing the confidence intervals of the current results.
vii) Multiple features might explain the same physiological traits. A log-likelihood ratio test should
be performed on all features where it is suspected that they are explaining the same thing - thus reduc-
ing the number of important features once more, and making the final interpretation easier.
But what can we use these results for, you ask?
It seems that some brain waves are more significant than others, when predicting survival. One way to
build upon the findings of this research is, therefore, to evaluate ’why’ something is significant. Multiple
studies have shown that it is possible to induce visual or audio stimulation, in order to activate certain
cells working at specific frequencies [70]. If the underlying problem for an increase or decrease in a
power band could be unveiled, it is possible that a similar approach would increase survival time. I.e.
inducing tones ranging from 14.5 to 33.5 Hz throughout the night might increase survival.
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
Eileen Leary suggested in 2020 that the amount of REM sleep was the best predictor for mortality in
all sleep stages. While we came to a similar conclusion, we also found that many other features are
better at explaining mortality than REM sleep.
The aim of this thesis was to understand which characteristics are present in people who survive
longer, using nocturnal polysomnography data. This involved extracting the frequency content of EEG,
EOG, and EMG, as well as temporal features conveying information about entropy, energy, and statis-
tical features (and more) for EEG, EMG, ECG, PPG, and SaO2 data. A combination of interpretable
deep learning models using the SHapley Additive exPlanations framework and Cox Proportional Haz-
ards model was used to evaluate the frequency domain.
The SHAP allowed for a high-resolution
interpretation of frequencies of significance. The SHAP results provided information that made it pos-
sible to split the power bands into segments, which the Cox model analyzed. In contrast to the SHAP,
the Cox model also allowed for the statistical interpretation of the results. The Cox model was also
selected for the ability to interpreting the temporal characteristics of the PSG. Both the frequency
features and temporal features were split into sleep stages. This allowed for an interpretation of which
sleep stages were highly important and what specific features were important across all sleep stages.
A total of 657 features were evaluated, and 132 features were determined to affect mortality when
considering known mortality risks. Honorable mentions are EEG N3 in the frequency range of 0-0.5,
0.75 to 2.75 Hz and REM in 14.75-33.5 Hz. EEG N3 0 - 0.5 Hz had the highest overall importance,
and resulted in a concordance index of 77.79%. It showed that for every 1 standard deviation increase,
the hazard increases by 14%. The EEG REM 14.75 - 33.5 Hz had a concordance index of 77.72%, and
a hazard ratio of 0.87 - suggesting that 1 std increase in REM is protective for mortality with 13%.
In a physiological relation, the study also suggests that increased sleep spindles and k-complexes are
protective for survival.
A general finding is that specific frequency bands of the EEG and EOG channels are informative
regarding survival.
One of the greatest findings is the 525 features that were not significant when
adjusting for confounders, and other than the EEG features, many temporal findings did not differ
between sleep stages. Thereby remarkably reducing the information needed to understand the effect on
survival.
The study opens up multiple new research areas. One of which would be to understand why, i.e.,
EEG REM 14.75-33.5 Hz is significant. If specific cells are working at these frequencies, it could be
possible to activate them through auditorial stimulation while sleeping. A similar approach for the
other features is possible.
In conclusion, this study has laid out the groundwork for understanding where to look for eﬀicient
predictors of survival.

segments are then cleaned up dependent on their duration. The final eye movement vector is seen in
second to the bottom.
